‚Äî *Some hopped-up, bush-watching, self-proclaimed leader of men*

# Side 1: Alice
## Track 1: Runaway Girl 
*DJ Jonah here, coming to you LIVE on the air from a SECRET LOCATION up in the BIRD'S NEST, overlooking a beautiful valley which shall NOT BE NAMED‚Ä¶ and let me tell you, listener JANE and listener JOHN‚Ä¶ let me tell you‚Ä¶ it's overrun with birds today, green ones and white ones, flocking together, going their own way‚Ä¶ Did you know the ancient ROMANS, Caesar himself, would read the future by the flight of the birds? Anyway, here's The CHORDETTES with LOLLIPOP‚Ä¶*

*Ba-Boom Boom-Boom*‚Ä¶  
‚Äã        ==It's June 13th, 1958, a Saturday, one month before my 18th birthday, one day before Elvis died.== It's an early-summer morning. The windows in my childhood bedroom are open, inviting smells of fresh cut grass and fat, lazy bumblebees. I‚Äôm wearing a baby-blue, sleeveless cotton sundress and white Keds. A record is playing on the little turntable I got for my birthday. I‚Äôm dancing with myself, twirling and bowing to an invisible suitor ‚Äî keeping one eye on my bedroom door, lest it should suddenly open.


*(Gee, my lollipop is‚ÄîHOOOOONK\!)*

I peer through the window overlooking the street and Cindy's brother's Chevy, red with white top and sides, is parked beside our lawn.  
‚Äã        "Be quiet\!" I say, laughing, waving my hands at them to pipe down. I lift the needle from the little record,


*(Lolli-SCRATCH\!)*


and stuff some necessities into my handbag: a little powder kit, my babysitting fortune of $17.62, and, after giving it some thought, a change of panties.  
‚Äã        "We'll all be late, Peaches!" yells Cindy from the car. I shoo at her and climb down the old Elm tree planted beneath my bedroom window and run across my parents' front lawn, stealthy and undetected. I slip into the back seat.   
‚Äã        Inside it's full of people and already steaming hot. Ricky Nelson is singing on the radio about having been a little fool. Cindy is stunning in a coral dress in the back seat, blonde and blue-eyed and radiating sunshine. I hug her and she says quietly, "I've got something to‚Äì"  
‚Äã        "Peaches\!"  
‚Äã        "Don't call me that," I say to Barry, who‚Äôs sweating at the wheel in a brown suit that looks too big for him.   
 ‚Äã        "Who's she?" says the girl sitting next to Cindy without looking in my direction. She‚Äôs got black cigarette pants and a black turtleneck and black sunglasses and black flat shoes. She‚Äôs reading through *Seventeen* (‚ÄúThe Big FASHION Issue\!‚Äù)   
‚Äã        "Oh," says Cindy, storing her secret. "I want you to meet my favorite cousin.‚Äù Her voice, warm and loud and Southern, takes on its Party-Hostess quality.   
‚Äã        "Eleanore," says the cousin over the magazine. Cindy lays a hand on Eleanore's knee and says, "After we‚Äôre done, we're taking Eleanore back to the train station going home. She lives in *New-York-City*, you know". Eleanore winces.  
‚Äã        "We've met before," I tell Eleanore.  
‚Äã        "Have we?" she says, disinterested. She pushes Cindy's hand away without looking up from her magazine.  
‚Äã        "Alright, boys and girls, here we go," says Barry. We jerk in our seats as the old jalopy springs into action. 

Sitting shotgun is a mystery boy, tall, very handsome, ginger-haired. He‚Äôs wearing a tight, white tee-shirt and has his hand resting limply behind Barry‚Äôs back. I give Cindy an important look...   
‚Äã        "Oh," says Cindy, her voice resuming that Party Hostess quality, "this of course is Barry's friend Tim". She whispers in my ear, "he has *all* the records". To Tim she says, "please meet Peaches. She‚Äôs a music fan, too".  
‚Äã        "Don't call me that," I whisper to her.  
‚Äã        ‚ÄúSorry,‚Äù she whispers back.   
‚Äã        "It's, eh, it's nice to meet you," I say to Tim.  
‚Äã        "So what's your real name, then?" he says. From his seat, right in front of me, he can only look at me through the rearview mirror. In the little frame I see his shades sitting atop his beautiful nose. He has a deep voice, deeper than Barry's, and he's wearing a very tight tee-shirt.  
‚Äã        "It's, ah," I say, gracefully, "it's Peggy Sue".  
‚Äã        Cindy and Barry snort with laughter. "Tell him your real name, Peaches," says Cindy.  
‚Äã        "It's Allison," I whisper.  
‚Äã        "What? Alice?" says Tim, already looking away. It seems Cindy's betrayal has cost me what little attention he had for me.  
‚Äã        "Yeah, I guess," I mumble. "Alice would do".  
‚Äã        "My little Wonderland girl," Cindy laughs, holding my hand. I instantly forgive her. "Here's to Alice\!"  
‚Äã        Barry and Tim and Eleanore join in a choir: "Alice\!"  
‚Äã        We all laugh. Somebody turns up the volume‚Ä¶


*(Splish splash\! I was having a bath\!)*

Scenic ==Greensboro rolls across the windows: the trimmed green lawns and rustic red stone of College Park.== "Now, Alice, I'm responsible for you," says Barry. ‚Äã        "Of course," I lie. "Mamma sends her regards".

‚Äã        Cindy presses against me. I look to my left: Eleanore and Cindy are sitting a foot apart. Both are locking sights on Tim. It suddenly feels even hotter in the car.

By the time we reach the Shell Durham Service Station (LARRY'S JUKE JOINT ADJACENT\!) ===it's so hot out that even the tarmac is sweating a little. We can see== a couple of peeling pumps, previously red, the workshop behind the office, where a carcass of a car lays hopelessly dead.  
‚Äã        Both Barry and Tim are drenched with sweat. Barry took off his coat and tie and he‚Äôs leaning on Tim, breathing hard; to our communal delight, Tim has taken off his shirt and is using it to dry his face. We girls leave the car ‚Äì to get fresh air, of course.  
‚Äã        "Fill 'er up?" says a stocky man in overalls, who appeared as if out of nowhere. His name patch says his name is Max.

‚Äã        "Do you need supervision fixing the car?" Eleanore says to Tim. She is watching him putting on his shirt.  
‚Äã        "Come, sister,‚Äù says Barry. ‚ÄúLet‚Äôs find us a mechanic‚Äù. ‚Äã        Cindy takes my hand, so I come, too. 


Larry's Juke Joint is a shack not far from, but certainly not adjacent to, the gas station. We walk through a swinging door and inside it‚Äôs one small room, cool and dark, smelling of stale beer and tobacco smoke. The sawdust on the floor is trampled and black, indicating that at other times the place is packed with dancers and drinkers. Now, in the morning, it‚Äôs empty save for a few dirty tables and an old jukebox. The jukebox is loudly playing a record I know ‚Äî "Wake Up, Little Susie", by the Everly Brothers. Next to the jukebox, at the only full table, two men sit next to two glasses and a half-empty bottle.  
‚Äã        "Please, no more," says the older one. He looks at his glass, nauseated.   
‚Äã        "What‚Äôs the matter with you?" says the younger man. "I thought you said you could drink". He's got a foreign accent and he's wearing a mechanic's overalls. The embroidered patch on his chest says his name is Eddie.  
‚Äã        We come closer.  
‚Äã        "Excuse me," says Barry, official-like. The man whose name is probably Eddie looks up. He's got black eyes and black hair, combed up like a wall above his head, and a clean shaven face. He's striking: not handsome, rather sharp-featured, keen.  
‚Äã        "If you're looking for Larry, you gotta holler through the kitchen door," he says. "He's in there making his terrible barbecue".  
‚Äã        "Actually," says Barry, "we need a mechanic".  
‚Äã        "Tough luck. He's not in today".  
‚Äã        "You're wearing a mechanic's uniform," says an indigent Barry and tugs at his tie. I giggle and Cindy presses my hand hard.   
‚Äã        "Just keeping his shirt warm. Come on," he says to the sallow-looking fellow next to him, "one last round. I'll give you triple or nothing".  
‚Äã        "No more, Eddie," says the sallow man. "Please, no more". He looks at his glass again ‚Äì it's about one eighth full ‚Äì and burps thoughtfully.  
‚Äã        "Excuse me very much," says Barry. "We have a vehicle in urgent need of repair".  
‚Äã        "Oh," says the mechanic. "I didn't know it was a *vehicle*. I thought you just had *car trouble*. In that case, I am *forlorn* to inform you that this very Thursday happens to be the National Day of Appreciation For the Italian-American Partnership, and *in light* of that, and the fact that Max just told me that I'm a slob, and at that a lazy one, our *vehicle repair services* are shut down for the day.‚Äù   
‚Äã        The other man laughs ‚Äì a drunkard's laugh, interrupted by belches. He then proceeds to crash head-first into the table. Moments later he's snoring.   
‚Äã        Barry looks confused. "Eddie," he says at last. "Can I call you Eddie?"

## Track 3: Dare to Dance  
*Mamas and daddies, sisters and friends ‚Äî hold that dial. After the weather we got a hand-clapper to lift your load... And what a morning this promises to be, with the Henrico County Fair Achievement Day already under way. This is your man, Tiger Tom, coming for WANT-AM Richmond and today we are LIVE from the fair, with fantastic guests already lining up telling us all about this special day of celebration. And what music shows will we have at the fair today, culminating with our guests from faraway Nashville, the Seven-Tops‚Ä¶ Anyway, here‚Äôs Jerry Butler & the Impressions with For Your Precious Love...*

*Step right up!*
‚Äã        We‚Äôre walking under a giant arched marquee welcoming us to the Henrico County Fair (June 4th-12th, 13th Achievement Day) and stepping right up into the fair. It all hits me at once: the *step right up*s and the *come on down*s of the carneys and the *whizz* and *clank-clank-clank* and *ahhhh*s and *mommy!* s of the carousel, the sizzle of meats on grills, the crunch of peanut shells under foot, the din of the crowd. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúDaddy, I want you to get me a Fairy Floss!‚Äù 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúDarnel sure looks fancy in that green coat.‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúWhat are these white kids doing here?‚Äù
‚Äã        And over on the PA:
‚Äã        ‚ÄúWill the child Bernard Green, Bernard Green, report to the manager‚Äôs office near the entrance. Bernard Green, your mother is waiting for you.‚Äù
‚Äã        And, then:


*(take out the papers and the trash!)* 


I say, ‚Äúit‚Äôs all so alive!‚Äù
‚Äã        Tim says, ‚ÄúAlright, Peggy Sue, don‚Äôt embarrass us in front of the cool cats‚Äù. 
‚Äã        Eddie says, ‚ÄúSince when do you know any cool cats?‚Äù
‚Äã        We walk through the midway, moving between pockets of PA sound. We stare at the stalls selling Friend Chicken and funnel cakes and pulled pork sandwiches and at the kids enjoying themselves on the Tilt-a-Wheel and the merry-go-round. I watch the midway, bustling with a huge throng of people, young parents with babies and old people and gangs of teenagers like us, all black. When we pass by them we get curious, reserved looks; people keep their distance. We are in a bubble in the crowd. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúBet you want a ride on the merry-go-round, Peaches,‚Äù says Barry. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúWe said we don‚Äôt call me that, *Bartholimio Joseph*,‚Äù I say, ‚Äúand as a matter of fact, I would *very much* like to go on the merry-go-round.‚Äù I give Tim a look I hope will convey forlonging, a word I learned from a book. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúWell, tough luck, *Alice*, we‚Äôre here to do a job and go home.‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúDo a job,‚Äù says Cindy, ‚Äúthen watch the show, and *then*... you know." 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúRight,‚Äù says Tim. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúAnd before that,‚Äù says Eleanore, ‚Äúmaybe watch the biggest pumpkin awards. You guys got that down here, right?‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúVery funny, city girl.‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúAnd no talking to strangers, I mean it,‚Äù says Barry. 
‚Äã        A stranger walks by, says, ‚ÄúWho wanna talk to *you*?‚Äù and walks away. 


Over a prominent double-width stall, between a barbecue stall and a ring-toss stall, stands the white banner and hexagonal logo of Buzz Cigarettes. We lean on an empty booth‚Äôs stall and watch the pretty girls in the ugly yellow dresses, giving away samples. 
‚Äã        ‚Äú‚Ä¶I enlisted them myself,‚Äù says Barry. ‚ÄúAll college girls, from Bennet College back at Greensboro, all polite, fine, fully capable of representing the Buzz Cigarettes brand‚Ä¶‚Äù  
‚Äã        ‚ÄúAll polite, fine,‚Äù mimics Eleanore, ‚Äúgiving four quarts of milk a day‚Ä¶‚Äù  
‚Äã        ‚ÄúShut up, city girl,‚Äù says Barry, ‚Äúmy father says this initiative just might make me the youngest junior executive ever in Buzz history.‚Äù  
‚Äã        The PA says, ‚ÄúWill the child Bernard Green please report to the manager‚Äôs office next to the entrance, Bernard Green, Bernie, your mom is worried.‚Äù
‚Äã        Eddie takes out a soft pack of Buzz cigarettes from his overalls pocket. ‚ÄúJunior executive,‚Äù he says and lights one up, ‚Äúnow, there‚Äôs something to aspire to.‚Äù  
‚Äã        Barry yanks the cigarette away from Eddie‚Äôs mouth. ‚ÄúWhy,‚Äù he says and puts it in his own mouth, ‚Äúwhat do you aspire to?‚Äù he coughs.   
‚Äã        Eddie reaches out and takes the cigarette back. ‚ÄúMe? I want the normal things. Success, fame‚Ä¶‚Äù he puffs on the cigarette, then offers it to Eleanore. ‚Äú‚Ä¶equality between races‚Ä¶‚Äù  
‚Äã        Eleanore puffs on the cigarette with an elegant city-girl air. ‚ÄúEquality between races," she says, "independence‚Ä¶ working for the United Nations‚Ä¶‚Äù she hands the cigarette to Tim. 
‚Äã        Tim takes it between his thumb and forefinger, gives it a very cool drag. ‚ÄúIndependence,‚Äù he says and puffs. ‚ÄúDriving my bike very fast‚Ä¶ napping in the sun‚Ä¶ beer‚Ä¶‚Äù  
‚Äã        Eddie looks at him. ‚ÄúI may grow to appreciate you, boy,‚Äù he says. Tim puffs in his face and gives the cigarette back to Barry.   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúI‚Äôm going for independence *and* watching my very first live show,‚Äù I say. ‚ÄúCan I have the cigarette?‚Äù

‚Äã        ‚ÄúIndependence,‚Äù says Cindy. She‚Äôs looking at her brother‚Äôs back. ‚ÄúThat sounds pretty good‚Äù. 
‚Äã        Barry says to Eddie, ‚Äúare these my cigarettes?‚Äù
‚Äã        Eleanore says, ‚ÄúAnyone else worried about Bernard Green?‚Äù


‚ÄúExcuse me, sir, may I interest you in a *honey* of a smoke?‚Äù  
‚Äã        The cigarette girl is young, lithe, wearing a yellow-and-white uniform proudly featuring the yellow hexagons of the Buzz Cigarettes brand. Her hat is a particularly ugly, yellow pillbox, with the Buzz logo sewn on it, like a military insignia. White straps around her neck hold a yellow plastic tray displaying the many products offered by the Buzz Cigarettes Co. She‚Äôs holding on to it with two hands covered in white gloves that make her look like a cartoon bee. Looking at her feels a lot like stepping into Cindy‚Äôs and Barry‚Äôs house.   
‚Äã        Barry stands erect and proud. With thumb and forefinger he‚Äôs smoothing an invisible mustache. Eddie tries to keep a straight face, but not trying too hard. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúAs you can see,‚Äù says the cigarette girl, ‚Äúwe have for you today the best tobacco products money can buy: fine Domestic Virginia cigarettes, and for the man who knows his smoke, we also offer Turkish Blend cigarettes, and an assortment of snuffs and chewin‚Äô tobaccis. Would you care for a free sample?‚Äù  
‚Äã        ‚ÄùChewing tobaccos,‚Äù says Barry, very sternly. ‚ÄúNot chewin‚Äô tobbacis. When you represent the Buzz brand, it is important that you keep to decorum.‚Äù  
‚Äã        ‚ÄùAre you tellin‚Äô me how to sell?‚Äù asks the cigarette girl, cocking her head at him, more curious than angry.   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúWell,‚Äù says Barry, ‚ÄúI *am* your boss.‚Äù   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúOh,‚Äù says the cigarette girl. ‚ÄúThen you must be Mr. B. J. MacAllister.‚Äù She turns to us and gives me a very inappropriate wink and adds, ‚Äú...junior.‚Äù  
‚Äã        ‚ÄúI am,‚Äù says Barry, and starts arranging items on her tray. She puffs her bosom, letting him. ‚ÄúThen you should know,‚Äù she says, ‚Äúthat so far I‚Äôve given out *forty-two* samples and had to restock *three times*. And it‚Äôs only an hour and a half past noon. So there.‚Äù

‚Äã        He lifts a loose cigarette from the tray. It has a hand-rolled kind of look and the sun going through the thin paper wrapper makes the content look more green than brown. The cigarette girl‚Äôs expression changes. I find it hard to decode whether she‚Äôs stone-faced or just terrified.   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúThis is completely unacceptable\!‚Äù says Barry. ‚ÄúCompletely\! Why, look at this‚Äì‚Äù we all look at the uplifted cigarette, like a sword ‚Äì ‚Äúas you can plainly see, this cigarette wrapper does not showcase the hexagon design nor the name ‚ÄòBuzz\!‚Äô‚Äù  
‚Äã        The cigarette girl is relieved. So is Eleanore, who gives her a private look.   
‚Äã        Then a heavy hand lands on Barry‚Äôs shoulder‚Äì  
‚Äã        ‚ÄúIs this Mr. B. MacAllister, Jr., I see before me?‚Äù says the owner of the heavy hand behind us.   
‚Äã        Eleanore acts fast: she grabs the green cigarette from Barry‚Äôs raised hand and stuffs it in his coat pocket. We all turn around to find two besuited, bespectacled, respectable-looking, identical-looking white men.    
‚Äã        Barry‚Äôs expression changes from shock to serenity, smoothes itself out so fast: ‚ÄúHello, Mr. Farley\! Why, gang, this is Mr. Farley, our advertising executive\! Mr. Farley, please meet my sister, my cousin and their friend.‚Äù

‚Äã        ‚ÄùIs that *right*?‚Äù says Mr. C. Evans.

‚Äã        ‚ÄúAny Buzz smoker is a welcome member of the Buzz family,‚Äù says Mr. Farley. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúWhy,‚Äù says Barry, eager to make his point, ‚Äúour representative here ‚Äî he shoves the unwilling cigarette girl into the spotlight ‚Äî ‚Äúhas already given out *forty-two* samples and had to restock *three times*. And it‚Äôs only an hour and a half past noon. And she is only one of our fine, fine‚Ä¶‚Äù 
‚Äã        ‚ÄùEdwardo!‚Äù  
‚Äã        The voice is fierce, low, piercing through the racket. It is soon thereafter joined by the biggest body I‚Äôve ever seen ‚Äî a tall, fat black guy in overalls ‚Äî moving swiftly towards us. I notice with interest that the only ones who stops to flinch is Eleanore and Mr. C. Evans.

‚Äã        Cindy says, ‚ÄúHappy to meet you, Mr. Evans, Mr. Farley.‚Äù She takes my hand and we run away as fast as we can.


A few people are sitting on folding chairs next to a big tent. Eddie and the huge man are walking towards them, with us as a rear guard. As Eddie gets closer to the group, they all jump from their seats, shouting:
‚Äã        ‚ÄúYou son of a bitch!‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúIs that Edwardo or am I seein‚Äô things?‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúBoy, you have some nerve!‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúAlright, everybody alright," says Eddie, "relax, won‚Äôt ya? I missed you guys too.‚Äù He hugs the pretty lady who‚Äôs standing there, saying nothing. She hugs him back but continues not to talk. Then Besame Mucho hugs him again. Then they all hug and pat him on the shoulder. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúAh,‚Äù says Eddie, ‚Äúthese are Alice and her friends. These unwitting sages, Alice most of all, informed me you sons of bitches have finally broke loose.‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúThen you have done us a great service,‚Äù says another man in a deep foreign accent. He has his hand around Eddie‚Äôs shoulder. I‚Äôve never seen a man dress like that, a ceremonial garb of another people, perhaps ‚Äî a white cotton dress, full of red and purple embroideries of flowers and birds. His cap is made from the same cloth. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúAnd these,‚Äù says Eddie, ‚Äúare some of my old friends. I don‚Äôt want to scare you, Alice, but some of these people are actual, factual members of the Steam Pillars‚Äù.        
‚Äã        I look at them: Eddie, Besame Mucho, the foreign man, the pretty woman ‚Äî small, voluptuous, with short curly hair and big earrings, looking like a down-to-Earth Dorothy Dandridge. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúWhat do musicians look like?‚Äù I ask. They laugh. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúGood answer, good answer.‚Äù

‚Äã        ‚ÄúHello, Babatunde,‚Äù I say. ‚ÄúI think you‚Äôre a musician, because other jobs won‚Äôt allow you to dress like that.‚Äù
‚Äã        Babatunde laughs. ‚ÄúVery good, Alice. I am a percussionist with the band. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúWhat does percussionist mean?‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúMeans he ain‚Äôt no drummer,‚Äù bellows Besame Mucho. 
‚Äã        Babatunde laughs, a hearty laugh.

‚Äã        ‚ÄúAll I‚Äôm saying,‚Äù says Eddie, ‚Äúis after the Steam Pillars‚Ä¶ I don‚Äôt think there‚Äôs going to be any of another show.‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúAre you being metaphorical?‚Äù Asks Cindy. ‚ÄúHave everybody met? I‚Äôm Cindy and this is my brother Barry and my cousin from New York City Eleanore.‚Äù
‚Äã        I suddenly realize that apart from Eddie, all the people are black, and reflect briefly on how much I‚Äôve grown since this morning. I‚Äôm tingling with excitement. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúYou guys are real musicians!"
‚Äã        ‚ÄúJust cogs in the machine,‚Äù says Mucho. 


‚Äã==Barry comes over, looking very smug.

‚Äã        ==‚ÄúWell ‚Äî‚Äú== says Barry. Absentmindedly, he reaches for his coat pocket and takes out the hand-rolled cigarette he confiscated from the cigarette girl. He takes out a yellow Buzz Cigarettes matchbook and lights it.    
‚Äã        ‚ÄúBoy, that tastes real funny,‚Äù he says. He hands the cigarette to Tim, who puffs on it, then ==opens== his eyes real wide. Some of the band members laugh.

‚Äã        Barry takes the half-smoked cigarette. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúIs that hygienic?‚Äù I ask. For some reason, everybody laughs.
‚Äã        ==‚ÄúIs that the cold, *crisp taste of Coke* I see in a bottle before me?‚Äù Says Barry. ==
‚Äã        ‚ÄúSee?‚Äù Says Eleanore. ‚ÄúYou‚Äôre already better.‚Äù 


We walk around the fairgrounds, waiting for the show to start. We pass an ice cream stand, where a young girl is crying over a fallen cone. When we pass by, she stops crying and stares at us. 
‚Äã        I duck down beside the girl. "Hey, don't cry," I say. "Mommy will buy you another!"
‚Äã        The little girl's mommy comes over and pulls her daughter by the elbow, hurrying her along. 
‚Äã        "I *told* you not to talk to strangers," she tells her.
‚Äã        "So good to see a friendly face!"
‚Äã        The young black man clasping Tim on the back is dressed in a dandy straw fedora and a mint-green button down shirt. A big gold watch on his wrist is catching the midday sun. From my perch close to the ground I watch in awe as Tim gives the man a big hug, and as the mother and child both stare at them, slack-jawed.
‚Äã        "Pete, how the hell are ya, kid?" he says. "Gang, this is my friend Pete. We sometimes go dancing together back home."
‚Äã        "Man," Pete tells Tim, "this fair, I tellya... It's all *squares* coming along with their *mothers*. Wait, are these your friends?"
‚Äã        "So... see you at the Seven-Tops show?" says Tim, growing crimson. 
‚Äã        "You bet your bottom dollar." 
‚Äã        Eleanore draws closer to Tim, and Cindy watches that with disdain. "Tim," she says, "I didn't know you were *with it*." 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúTim,‚Äù says Cindy, ‚Äúwill you win me a teddy?‚Äù
‚Äã
At the next stall, under the string of bare lightbulbs, the hand-painted sign at the nearby booth says ‚Äú10 shots \- 25¬¢,‚Äù and the red-faced man in the open-collared shirt under it is busy counting shots of .22 into the hand of a young black patron, who‚Äôs chatting excitedly with his girlfriend. A bunch of old Winchester rifles lie at the counter. The man is picking up one of them and shooting into the depths of the booth, where metal cutouts, shot to tatters, are supposed to imitate ducks and quails. 
‚Äã        Every time a rifle fires, the red-faced man jumps.  
‚Äã        ‚ÄúExcuse me,‚Äù says Cindy, ‚Äúthis young man would like to win me the largest teddy bear you have.‚Äù  
‚Äã        ‚ÄùThat would be the Bear-gineer,‚Äù says the red-faced man, wiping a handkerchief across his sweaty forehead. He gestures towards the top shelf above his head, where a large bear, wearing a train engineer‚Äôs hat and overalls, is sitting. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúGotta warn you, young man, 12 days so far and no one won him yet.‚Äù The man next to us fires a round into the line of automated duck-like things and it spins around its axis with a *ping\!*  
‚Äã        The red-faced man shudders.   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúWhat do I need to do to win?‚Äù says Tim and puts a quarter on the counter. The man at the counter counts ten shots into his hand.   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúTen shots at the ducks and quails,‚Äù says the man and jumps at another ping\!, ‚Äúand I‚Äôll be counting‚Äù.   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúThat will not be a problem,‚Äù says Tim, picks up a rifle and immediately drops half his shots to the ground.   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúHave you ever fired a rifle before?‚Äù asks Cindy.  
‚Äã        ‚ÄúSure have\!‚Äù says a blushing Tim. He pulls the lever on the old rifle and drops the rest of his shots. He ducks, takes one shot from the ground, cleans it against his shirt and puts it in the rifle. He smiles at me, trying to save face. Eleanore smirks. He aims, fires ‚Äî and takes the Bear-gineer‚Äôs eye out. The shot teddy flops in its place like an injured cowboy.   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúAt the ducks and quails\!‚Äù shouts the red-faced man. ‚ÄúNot the trophies\!‚Äù  
‚Äã        Tim smiles weakly at Cindy.   
‚Äã        Cindy says to Tim, ‚Äúwhy don‚Äôt you put another quarter on the counter?‚Äù And to the man at the counter, ‚Äúmind if I try?‚Äù  
‚Äã        ‚ÄùWhy not,‚Äù says the man, shuddering again, ‚Äúto be worser off than him‚Ä¶ you‚Äôd have to be *spectacularly bad.‚Äù*  
‚Äã        ‚ÄùSorry, Cind,‚Äù says Tim. ‚ÄúYou want me to try again?‚Äù  
‚Äã        ‚ÄùOh, you sweet, summer child,‚Äù says Eleanore.   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs okay, hon,‚Äù says Cindy. She winks at Eleanore. Eleanore catches the gesture, not quite sure what to do with it.   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúRemember we used to do these back in ‚ÄòBama?‚Äù  
‚Äã        ‚ÄùThat was a long time ago,‚Äù says Eleanore and walks away, towards Barry and his identical executive friends. But she looks back, somewhat appeased.   
‚Äã        Cindy turns around and picks up the old Winchester like a trained soldier. She sends her left leg back, like a ballet dancer, like an expert shot. She puts the rifle to her temple and I can tell that there‚Äôs a longstanding familiarity about it. I look back: Eleanore is behind me, watching too.
‚Äã        ‚ÄúTurn around, girl,‚Äù she says, ‚Äúyou‚Äôll miss the show‚Ä¶ it goes by awful quick.‚Äù  
‚Äã        I turn around just in time to watch Cindy squeezing the trigger, once, twice, ten times, barely breathing, and five ripped-metal ducks and five tattered quails going *ping!* -ing and spinning away. The red-faced man backs away. So do some of the patrons and competitors at the shooting booth. In this moment, even I can feel there‚Äôs something very much intimidating in soft, sweet, blue-eyed Cindy.    
‚Äã        Cindy puts her gun on the stand. The red-faced man reaches above him without taking his eyes off of her and gives her the one-eyed Bear-gineer. She smiles softly and gives it to Tim.   
‚Äã        Tim takes the teddy, all red. Some people laugh ‚Äî not many, though.   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúHey, hey,‚Äù he says to the faces around us. ‚ÄúI deserve it, right?‚Äù  
‚Äã        He smiles at Cindy and she grabs him and kisses him right on the mouth, right in front of everybody. I look back and catch Eleanore scowling. 


Cindy and I stand alone by the shooting booth. Life has resumed its course, ducks-a-pinging and the red-faced man a-jumping, but some people still avoid Cindy and me when they walk by, giving us a wide berth.   
‚Äã        ‚ÄúThat was a stupid thing I did,‚Äù says Cindy. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúWhat did you do, boo?‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúI angered Eleanore.‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúThought that was your plan all along.‚Äù
‚Äã        ‚ÄúYeah, but I think I overdid it. And she *is* my ticket out of here.‚Äù  
‚Äã        My eyes suddenly fill with tears and I can‚Äôt see anything and, to hide that, I‚Äôm looking at my shoes. 
‚Äã        ‚ÄúWhat do you mean, your ticket out of here?‚Äù I ask her shoes.

## Track 4: All Aboard! 
*‚Äú‚Ä¶And now we swing on over to local happenings, where the bright airs of the Henrico County Fair‚Äôs Achievement Day turned mighty stormy this afternoon... ‚Äã        We turn left at a little sign saying NINTH STREET VIADUCT - ERECTED M.C.M.XIII - GEORGE AINSLIE MAYOR, and find ourselves on a long, tall wooden bridge, flanked on both sides by the stormy waters of the George River. In the distance, grey chimneys shoot out from the horizon. The wheels rattle on the wooden planks of the bridge and we all vibrate. It gives a comical aspect to Cindy's earnestness. 
‚Äã        "I'm a young woman," says Cindy. She's looking at Barry with pleading eyes. "I need to live. I choke at home. I choke under his control. And ever since mom died... Barry, just thinking of going back home makes me unable to breathe."
‚Äã        Barry hangs his head, so low I worry he's fallen asleep at the wheel. He's quiet for a long while. After some time, Cindy lays a hand on his shoulder. 
‚Äã        "I am sorry," she says. "Please forgive me."
‚Äã        "I can't solve your problem," says Barry. 
‚Äã        "What do you mean?"
‚Äã        "I mean I'm just a boy," Barry tells her. "A boy who's still afraid of his father. I can't solve your problem. But sister -- before we have to go back home, how about we catch a show?"
‚Äã        Cindy's eyes fill with tears. She's nodding.
‚Äã        "Yeah?" says Barry.
‚Äã        "Yeah,‚Äã" says Cindy, smiling.
‚Äã        Barry slams on the brakes. Behind us a car horn goes off. Barry spins the wheel and we turn around. Cars, stuck on both directions, starts honking at us. 
‚Äã        "Sorry, sorry!" Barry says. "Family emergency."
‚Äã        He backs up, turns again, backs up again. 
‚Äã        "Learn to drive!" someone shouts at him. 
‚Äã        Barry breaks free and steps on the gas. The wheels rattle on the wooden bridge. 
‚Äã        "We have to get Eleanore before she gets on the train," says Cindy. 
‚Äã        "Set sail for Eleanore!" says Barry.
‚Äã        We all cheer.
‚Äã        "Someone turn on the radio!" I say.

## Track 5: Second Street Saturday Night

## Track 6: Birds of a Feather 
- [ ] ==radio break
*...and that was XXXX by the XXX, as requested by listener XXX... who tells us it was the first song she heard being played live. Do you remember your first show?*==

We cross the street in solemn silence. 
‚Äã        In the small parking lot beneath the Buzz warehouse, a few cars are parked, most of them old and dusty. As we walk between them I pass my finger through the dust on a Ford's back window. 
‚Äã        "Hello," I say, "What's this?"
‚Äã        Someone painted a giant bird, a cardinal or a jay, on the driver's door. We stop to admire the painting. 
‚Äã        "Why would someone devalue their own car like that?" Barry wonders. 
‚Äã‚Äã        ‚ÄãEleanore snorts.
‚Äã        "It's beautiful," I say.
‚Äã        Another car, a bluebird painted on its trunk lid and its headlights burning through the darkened lot, turns and parks. In the headlights I can see paintings on some other cars -- birds and butterflies, clouds and railroad tracks. The lights turn off. A woman, black in a long, white coat, bounds out. She checks her wristwatch as she's shutting the door. She looks up, straight at me, and smiles. Then she disappears into the shadow of the warehouse. 
‚Äã        "Fellow fan?" Eleanore asks. 
‚Äã        "Let's follow her," I say. 
‚Äã        We do.


At the foot of the giant building, the huge front entrance is locked. A night-guard sits snoring next to the door. We see the hem of a white coat disappearing in the alley.
‚Äã        "There she is," I whisper. 
‚Äã        "Why are you whispering?" whispers Cindy. 
‚Äã        We follow the white coat. 


The alley is ancient and dusty and has many doors and openings to the left and right. The thin moonlight reflects off the granite cobblestones. Someone hung lanterns all down the alley. Some are lit, some not... The woman in the white coat opens a door next to an unlit lantern and disappears into it. 
‚Äã        "Wait, you guys," I whisper. "I want to do something."
‚Äã        I raise the little glass hatch of the unlit lantern next to the door. With my last remaining Buzz Cigarettes match, I light it. In the light of the lantern I can see someone painted a big, black X on the door, with red where the lines cross.
‚Äã        "Come on, Wonderland girl, it's panty time," Cindy whispers. Eleanore snickers. Tim opens the little door. 


I go down a long, narrow staircase, careful in this darkness to hold on to the metal railing. At the bottom another door opens to a darkened cathedral of a warehouse, redolent of musty tobacco, and we enter it just as the white figure crosses between two bales of huge leaf. 
‚Äã        "There she is," whispers Tim. "To your left."
‚Äã        Passageways wind between the tall wobbly towers of tobacco. 
‚Äã        ‚Äã"They built a maze," Eleanore says. "Who are these people?" 
‚Äã        At some point we are lost in the maze, but then a white hem appears again. We scurry after the figure, quietly.
‚Äã        "Take a left," says Tim.
‚Äã        And then:
‚Äã        "Here she is again. She went between these two crates."
‚Äã        And then:
‚Äã        "I think we're close."


*(I mean, at the last show Heath played this hopping number--listen, man...)*


Up ahead we hear voices chatting excitedly, smell burning tobacco against the backdrop of wet leaf. Someone says something and someone laughs and as we reach a little clearing among the tobacco towers, we see our white-coat girl as she's swallowed by a little crowd. 
‚Äã        We stand in a little clearing bordered by tall tobacco towers, a little bubble far away from the crowd. In the dim light I see the most extravagant group of people I've ever seen: men in Pompadour haircuts wearing denim jackets and women in poodle skirts, long and multicolored and bearing appliqu√©s of rails and trains; here a white boy in a vest adorned with badges, like military medals, is hugging a black girl with pin curls in her hair and a pencil skirt revealing her knees; there two guys in oversized Zoot suits, looking like mini Elvises, are trying to chat up two girls in circle skirts and large, buckled belts.
‚Äã        "First she says you can do a steam pillar, then she doesn't want a steam pillar," says a voice behind me. I turn around with a grin.
‚Äã        "Eddie!" I say happily.
‚Äã        "I mean the whole name of the band, *Madonna*, is The Steam Pillars," says Eddie as he's grinning at me from atop a mess of plugs and wires. 
‚Äã        "Eddie," says Barry, unhappily. 
‚Äã        "So the corner-dancing troupe has arrived," says Eddie. He connects two wires and sparks fly out, illuminating his face. "Hi, Mr. MacAllister," he says, "I see you're still wearing your corporate uniform." 
‚Äã        Barry scowls. 
‚Äã        "What are you building?" I ask.
‚Äã        "Never mind what am I building. Let's just say it's awesome and you'll never get to see it. First she says go ahead, Eddie, build your steam pillar, then all of a sudden--" he turns to me, earnestly: "Tell me, when did safety become such an issue at a *rock and roll concert?*" 
‚Äã        "What's a steam pillar, Eddie?" Eleanore says, clenching and un‚Äãclenching her fists.
‚Äã        "What are you kids still doing here?" says Eddie. "Go on, scatter. You're in the show now. Go mix with the crowd. Dance. This is what you were chasing."
‚Äã        ‚ÄãI hold Cindy's hand as we walk into the crowd.
‚Äã        "We're in the show now," I tell her. "This is it." She squeezes my hand. Someone whistles. The lights go out.


A single spotlight falls on a little stage on the other side of the clearing, illuminating The Steam Pillars. At the back of the stage Babatunde starts drumming on an array of little drums, an ancient, alien beat... and then bam! The rest of the band joins in, a wall of happy music, electric guitar and electrifying piano. The crowd erupts in applause. I find myself immediately and without thinking of it engaged in dancing, moving with the crowd. 
‚Äã        "I see a lot of new faces here today!" Vilma says into the microphone. Even from across the room she's stunning in a grey-green evening dress. 
‚Äã        "We're new faces!" I scream. Eleanore and Cindy hoot.
‚Äã        Vilma says, "And so many familiar faces, too!"
‚Äã        I look around, to see who in the crowd is a seasoned fan and who's a greenhorn. At the back of the room, I see Eddie still fussing and cussing over his mess of electricity.
‚Äã        "How many of you, how many," says Vilma, "Have boarded the Steam Pillars train all the way back at the Coal Woodshed?" 
‚Äã        A couple of voices call and shout and the rest of the crowd laughs.
‚Äã        "Who here came on board in Charleston? Who in Raleigh?" asks Ruth, and more people sound off. "How many in Wilmington? How many came here from the Henrico county fair this morning?"
‚Äã        Cindy and I hoot. Eddie walks by, pushing his way through the crowd. He stops next to Barry, motioning for him to move.
‚Äã        ‚Äã"How many came straight from the Second Avenue demonstration?"
‚Äã        Eleanore shouts, "That's us too!"
‚Äã‚Äã        Eddie kneels at Barry's feet, pulling at some cables the way you pull turnips out of the ground. 
‚Äã        "What are you doing?" Barry shouts, politely.
‚Äã        "She says she doesn't want a steam pillar!" Eddie shouts back. "So I'm disassembling the steam pillar. First she says go ahead--"
‚Äã        "That's it!" Barry laughs. "Do what you're told. Conform!"
‚Äã        On stage, Vilma says, "And who here is ready, who here is willing! I say who here is ready and willing to ride this train?"
‚Äã        The little crowd roars in applause. The drums are alone now, their rhythm a slow churning of train wheels. 


*(CHACHA-chacha-CHACHA-chacha!)*


Now the guitarist ducks towards the amplifier and strums. The guitar vibrates in her hands. It seems to grow bigger. The air is now full with an electric whistle like a train horn, growing louder and louder. 
‚Äã        "You know what, you're right," Eddie yells over the siren. "Safety?" He pulls two wires close together. "What safety? We're dealing with *magic*!" He drops the wires to the ground. A whole mass of them starts whizzing and crackling. We all take a step back, then another, then, as the guitar-horn's sounds reaches deafening proportions, *whoosh!* -- a huge pillar of steam erupts from where we stood only a moment ago. The crowd is hooting and dancing around the geyser. Then the steam and the sound both ebb away. A mist of warm water covers my face. Cindy shouts in surprise and we both laugh.
‚Äã        "What did I tell you?" says Eddie. "Magic." 
‚Äã        As he turns to leave he winks at me and says, "Move closer to the stage, Alice of Wonderland. That's where magical things happen."


*(CHACHA-chacha-CHACHA-chacha!)*


Cindy and I shuffle our feet to the tempo of the wheels and it increases with every beat. The eruption of steam has cleared a little round clearing and people dance around it.  
‚Äã‚Äã        The railroad rhythm is strong now. The piano issues a familiar tune under the scorching whistle of the guitar. And suddenly it's like everybody around the little clearing is moving forwards; like this whole mass of people is pushed  on invisible wheels. Eleanore puts two fingers in her mouth and whistles and shouts, "All aboard!"


Vilma is singing, 


*(Thi-is train... is a clean train--)*
‚Äã

Around, the whole crowd's dancing strangely. No one is doing dances I know: no Jitterbugs or Strolls, no Bunny-hops or Boogie-Woogies.

‚Äã        "All of you except," Vilma says on stage, "for Eddie, who might be killed by Mama before the day is out."


I find myself drawing away from Cindy and into the embrace of the crowd. As the bodies move and wash over me I find myself swept with them and they carry me along, towards the stage, where magical things are told to happen. ‚Äã        "I think she's gonna do the nobody's fault number now," says a man next to me. I push forward.
‚Äã        The guitarist walks to the front of the stage now. She holds up her white guitar and plays a little tune -- a bumbling, limping one, soft and small. Around me I hear scattered applause. This, I decide, is the sound of the veteran fans. One veteran on the other side of the empty clearing yells, "It's nobody's fault but mine, Sister!" I push between groups of fans. ‚Äã        The guitar sounds again, playing the same little ditty. But now it's louder, angrier: I see the sound as it washes over the faces of the people around me, see heads bob up and down in agreement.  


At the foot of the stage, the site of magic, the crowd is thick and already moving together to the beat, like synchronized swimmers in colorful dresses and made-up hair. A kid with railway patches on his vest shouts something at the stage and Mucho shouts back. 
‚Äã        "Are you new here?"
‚Äã‚Äã        I turn my head. There, the lady in the white coat is dancing next to me. Up close, she's pretty; freckles cover her cheeks, around her nose, under a pair of shades. ‚Äã        "Are you new here?"
‚Äã        "I am," I say. "My name is Alice and this is my first show."
‚Äã        "A greenhorn," says the woman and suddenly grins. She throws her arms around me and says, "Would you care to dance?"
‚Äã        My heart thumps. "I sure would love to!" I shout, happy, when I realize we're *already* dancing.
‚Äã        Now the little tune coming from the stage is a hulking monster, a disjointed, angry cry, and the drums and bass start to thunder down on it. The crowd jumps and dances to the strange rhythm...
‚Äã        "My name is Shirley," she shouts. Her body sways along with the rhythm of the crowd. I try to match my movement with hers and fail. At last I settle on the Bunny-hop and Shirley nods in approval as she dances her weird dance. 
‚Äã        "Why did you paint on your car?" I shout.
‚Äã        "Pardon?"
‚Äã        "Why is there a painting on the hood of your car? The bird painting!"
‚Äã        Shirley smiles but doesn't answer. Then Vilma's voice fills the little space, bouncing and reverberating off the tall ceiling:


*(If I die and my soul gets lost...)*

‚Äã        "Coming down?" I ask Shirley. 
‚Äã        "Vilma is going to come to us," she shouts in my ear, "and this is the crucial part, greenhorn. In this show you're not a spectator. You need to join in."
‚Äã        "I'm not a what?"
‚Äã        "You need to join in," she says. "It's nobody's fault but mine!" she sings. The foot-of-the-stage crowd around us dances and sings with her. ‚Äã        "Shirley!" says a man with patches on his vest. "Come dance with us!" 
‚Äã        "Maybe we'll dance later," says Shirley. 
‚Äã        "I hope we will!"
‚Äã        She dances away. 


The pianist starts hammering at the piano. His sound -- dulcet, sweet, but rhythmically forceful -- competes with the scorching disarray of notes emanating from the guitar. It is incredibly loud up here, and each sound seems to bounce off the thick throngs of people, reverberate and multiply. I can feel Babatunde and Mucho in my chest. 
‚Äã        Now Vilma nods to Babatunde, and he changes his rhythm to one of tension and expectation. Vilma removes her microphone from its stand and climbs down from the little stage. Surrounded by the crowd she seems slight, almost tiny, but with every step she takes the crowd parts in front of her. She makes her way, microphone in hand and long cable dragging behind her, deep into the crowd. Now the drums issue a warning -- *TA-TA-ta-TAM!* -- and Ruth starts singing:


*(Nobody's fault but mine,
Nobody's fault but mine.
If I don't go back to my love tonight--)*


Vilma thrusts up the microphone in the face of a tall, good looking woman standing alone. The crowd waits in anticipation. The woman's face lights up. With all the air in her lungs she shouts into the microphone, "It's nobody's fault but mine!"


The crowd roars in applause. Someone puts her hand on the  woman's shoulder. Vilma moves on to sound of the the sweet and scorching music, dancing and swaying her hips as she does. She stands next to a worried looking man and singing, 


*(If I don't bring the dough back home--)*


The man issues a grimy, gravelly, off-tune, "It's nobody's fault but mine!" into the microphone, almost knocking back Vilma with its force. The crowd erupts. In the dim light coming from the stage I can see his eyes are misty. Vilma walks on, every now and then thrusting the microphone into somebody's face; in the distance I see her as she sings, 


*(If I choose which path I'll walk...)*

‚Äã        Vilma is walking back now, towards the stage. 
‚Äã        I wonder what my own voice would sound like, bouncing off the many eardrums in the room. As Vilma nears me I stand  in the middle of the empty path she left behind her. Vilma looks me in the eye and nods. She's standing right in front of me. Her eyes seem to scan me from top to bottom. My heart beats and for a moment it seems the music coming from the stage dies down. It's only Vilma and me in the dim light, and she sings, very close to me,


*(If I dance all by myself...)*


She puts her microphone to my face, her face a mask of expectation. I see the microphone cable snaking behind her, a long black line reaching all the way back to the stage. I breathe in, fully prepared to say it's all my fault that I'm over here dancing alone, without my Cindy. Then I think of Cindy going limp in my arms, signaling she's still leaving. But then I think of Cindy's face in the car, imploring Barry to listen, to hear that she can't breathe at home.
‚Äã        I find myself singing into the microphone, "It's nobody's fault..." and choke.
‚Äã        ‚ÄãVilma looks at me, expecting the last two words. But none come out. Then her face changes. She giggles. Then the audience laughs. My face grows hot. Then the audience cheers. *(It's nobody's fault! Damn right!)*


Strangers around me give me sympathetic looks. Someone is hugging me. I have found momentary stardom. I discover the woman hugging me to be Shirley. She whispers into my ear, "Not bad for a greenhorn!"
‚Äã        "You smell good," I whisper back. 
‚Äã        "What?"
‚Äã        The song comes to a huge, concluding riff... then a burst of piano notes... then quiet. The crowd, Shirley and I included, hoots and shouts and claps. Around me I see people crying -- not just the girls. 
‚Äã        "I have a secret," I say to Shirley as the music dies down.
‚Äã        "Tell me," she says. We're still hugging, and kind of still dancing. Even as the players are preparing quietly for the next song, I find that dancing just won't die down. Everybody around us is dancing a little to the sound of silence in the room. 
‚Äã        "I brought something to throw on stage," I say, "sort of like... you know... some proof that I've been here."
‚Äã        "Just don't tell me it's a pair of panties," she says, shaking her head.

‚Äã        "It's not that kind of show, Alice."‚Äã        
‚Äã        "It isn't?"
‚Äã        ‚ÄãBabatunde's drums slowly start filling the room. They're slow, strange, wildly appealing.
‚Äã        "Why did you paint a bird on your car, Shirley?"
‚Äã        On stage, Babatunde's palms come up and down on his drums and under the spotlight I see the dust dancing between his hands... the dust dances in patterns and shapes it's not supposed to assume...
‚Äã        "Why did you paint those birds?"
‚Äã        "You'll see!"
‚Äã        Then the lights go out on stage. 


We dance in total darkness. Every now and then I feel another body -- a hand touching mine, a torso pressing against mine. I don't know for how long I've been dancing and what The Steam Pillars are playing... I hear many voices, many instruments, many bodies moving across mine. I feel like I'm being carried by a stream of people, towards the center of the room, and also up, up... It really feels like I'm flying.
‚Äã        Now my eyes adjust to the dim light... or am I just thinking that I see it? But I'm in the middle of the cleared circle in the middle of the room, dancing, no longer the Bunny Hop, but a dance that feels to me to be much more ancient. I see people dance around me. I see my friends. I look at Barry as he's dancing frantically, as he's taking off his jacket. Now his jacket becomes a pair of wings. Now Barry is a cardinal, small and multicolored. He flaps his wings and flies away... I am flying too. I wonder what bird am I, what bird do I look like to the man dancing next to me. Piano notes, strong and urgent, come at me from somewhere...
‚Äã        Shirley's voice comes on in me ears now. But I can't see Shirley anywhere.
‚Äã        "Did you see it?" she shouts. "What did you see?"
‚Äã        "I see it now!" I shout back into the void. The piano notes in my ears become a procession of piano keys in my eyes... they march as if on parade... now the keys are transformed again...
‚Äã        "What do you see?"
‚Äã        "I see a caravan," I say. "A beautiful, long caravan."
‚Äã        The music is quiet now, slow. I see Shirley dancing with me, in the middle of the circle.
‚Äã        I look at her and see her face as if under a magnifying glass: every hair in her eyebrow and the hills and valleys in the yellow around her iris... her nose... her lips...
‚Äã        "You do smell good," I mutter. 
‚Äã        "You smell good too, Alice."
‚Äã        "It's Alice of Wonderland. Please."
‚Äã
It's dead quiet and the stage is empty. The room is lit. Somehow, everybody is still dancing, with force and energy as if the music was still thumping. I can't stop dancing, either. Shirley is gone... or was she ever here? I am surrounded by Barry, Tim, Eleanore and my Cindy. 
‚Äã        I'm struck by how different Barry looks now. I‚Äôm sure it‚Äôs not just the lack of coat and tie that makes him look so‚Ä¶ so beautifully loose‚Ä¶
‚Äã        I'm looking at Cindy and Eleanore hugging. Eleanore kisses Cindy's cheek. Tim and Barry are dancing very close now, locking sights.
‚Äã        I choke. "I love you guys," I say.
‚Äã        Cindy comes over and kisses me. The need to dance subsides. We hug, but we also still kind of swoosh and sway.
‚Äã        "I've been to my first show," I say to her. She looks at me and pats my cheek.
‚Äã        "You have, Alice of Wonderland," she says. "You have".
‚Äã        "This was the most beautiful moment of my life," I say. "And now I fear it's gone". I start crying and she hugs me and doesn't say anything. I think she's thinking the same thing.


After a while the dancing ebbs out, like the energy running out of a lightbulb‚Ä¶ first in a steady decline and then in sputters of sudden idleness. The thirty or so people in the room are looking at each other as if they all woke up together from a weird dream. Shy looks at first, but then looks that say *yes, I've seen what you've seen myself and you're not crazy and it did, in fact, happen, and by the way, brother or sister, how cool was that?* followed by a quiet, almost mute display of hand-shaking and cheek-kissing and shoulder-clasping‚Ä¶ someone at the back of the room shouts, "I love everybody here" and it doesn't sound strange. In fact, it sounds oddly and quietly true.
‚Äã        The thirty of us leave as a group.
‚Äã        "You don't have to go home," I say to Cindy as we wind our way through the tobacco maze. 
‚Äã        "What do you mean?"
‚Äã        "I figured it out. These guys have a *caravan*. They paint their cars to show they're part of the caravan."
‚Äã‚Äã        ‚Äã"They follow the band around?"
‚Äã        "I mean... instead of running away with Eleanore..."
‚Äã        "If Barry would only agree..."
‚Äã        "They paint things they saw. Things they saw during the show."
‚Äã        "What do you mean, things they saw?" asks Cindy, looking at me all askew.


In the parking lot, a few people are waiting for us: Eddie and the cigarette girl and five or six others, all holding bunches of bracelet and looking holy and filled with purpose. Eddie beelines towards me. I run to him and hug him and kiss his stubbly cheek.
‚Äã        "I've been to my first ever show, Eddie!"
‚Äã        ‚ÄãHe smiles broadly and hands me a bracelet. It's a simple, uncolored thread but it has two coins slotted on it. They jingle against one another in my hand.
‚Äã        "What's this for, Eddie?"
‚Äã        "For being, as I already predicted, young lady, chosen".
‚Äã        ‚ÄãThen his smile fades. He says, "we leave tonight for Fuckington".
‚Äã        "You sound like you're saying goodbye," I say. He says nothing, just turns away. He turns to another lady and gives her a big wide smile and hands her a braid and says, "we leave tonight for Fuckington".
‚Äã        All around me, Eddie's friends are handing us all braids and saying that they're leaving tonight for Fuckington.
‚Äã        I shout at Eddie, now far away, "Hey!"
‚Äã        "What, Alice of Wonderland?"
‚Äã        "How do you get chosen, Eddie?"
‚Äã        He smiles and walks away. All his friends are leaving, having depleted their stock of braids.
‚Äã        "You choose," he shouts back through the empty street. the cigarette girl throws her arm around his shoulder as they walk. "That's how you get chosen. You choose".
‚Äã        Then, for the first time since the show started, it's *actually* quiet.
‚Äã        Shirley is putting on the braid Eddie handed to her. I see she already has one. The coins from her two braids all jingle together.
‚Äã        "Will I see you in Fuckington?" she asks. I look at Barry with a sinking heart. Now that he's outside, breathing normal-people air again, what would he do?
‚Äã        "Will you paint on your car tonight, Alice?"
‚Äã        We stand there, all looking at each other.
‚Äã        Then this one guy walks over to his car. I think he's leaving but he bends over, reaches behind the wheel, pops his trunk open. He takes a big can of red paint and a brush and he's singing, singing to himself or to us,


*"I'm-a gonna paint me*  
*grRRREAT balls of fi-re*  
*I'm-a ‚Ä¶bout to paint me‚Ä¶"*


"Another bird joins the flock," says Shirley.


When he's finished, some other guy comes and takes the brush, turning towards his car. Somebody found a few more paintbrushes in his trunk and he puts them in the middle of the little lot. Some more paint cans are found ‚Äì candy-apple red, mint-green, midnight blue ‚Äì and then some aerosol spray cans, too. One girl paints a striking image of a Tropical Turquoise heart melting over her front door. Someone transforms his '52 Bel Air into a rainbow. A group of young workmen are busy filling every surface ‚Äì doors, windows, roof ‚Äì on their little van with all the lyrics they could remember from the show they'd just watched. They use paint, they use Magic Markers. They are humming the songs as they do that. I suddenly realize how filled with song that little car-lit parking lot has become. 
‚Äã
At that point I am hit by an image I will never forget: a flock of birds convening mid-flight, matching their speed, sharing their direction, and always changing, rearranging: a leader becoming a follower. A follower becoming a leader.
‚Äã‚Äã	I whisper to Cindy, "talk to him. Tell him. Tell him you'll be leaving either way".
‚Äã        But Cindy knows her brother better. She walks over to him and takes his hand. Suddenly they seem to me like they were when I first met them, these two kids from the country, holding each other‚Äôs hand in the recess ground of a big-city school.
‚Äã        Barry is holding hands with his sister, looking at the people in the clearing, and nodding -- as if to an unheard beat.
‚Äã        "Yes, yes," he says, maybe to his sister and maybe to himself. "Anyway, why the hell not?"
‚Äã        He walks towards the middle of the parking lot, where paint and brushes are waiting.


# Side 2: Tim
## Track 7: Morning, Gorgeous
*Do you remember your first time? Butterflies fluttering in your belly and sweat on your palms ‚Äì the world suddenly an alien place, shiny and foreign and beautiful ‚Äì the chirps of birds all of a sudden holding meaning beyond meaning? And what do I mean by ‚Äúfirst time,‚Äù listener? Am I being lude? Ludicrous? Out-ragous? Reb-belious? Am I talking about es-ee-ex? Maybe, listener, maybe I am. But there are all sorts of first times in our life and they start way back on that first step we take as tiny little toddlers. So cute\! So: do you remember your first strike on that baseball pitch? The first time your momma took you to the movies? And even ‚Äì yessiry indeed\! ‚Äì your very first kiss? More importantly, listener, and listen closely, buddy: do you remember the song that was playing at the time? Here‚Äôs a little number so many of us would recognize as the soundtrack for all sorts of first times ‚Äì those four angels from Sheboygan, the wonder-ful Chordettes, singing a plea for the Sandman himself‚Ä¶*

*(Pam-pam-papa-pam-pam-pam-pa-pa-pampam-pah‚Ä¶)*

I first fell in love with Alice at the sunrise after the night we decided to follow the band. Even years later, when an early summer sun would catch my eye just right, I would instantly see it again: how Alice was asleep in the back seat of Barry‚Äôs old Chevy, the angle of her head, slumped on the window, and those freckles on that pale skin, and how her fingers and the tips of her hair were dipped in radiant pink paint‚Ä¶  
Barry has been driving right behind the band‚Äôs van, and every now and again Eddie would pop his head out of the window and shout something back, something like, we stop for gas in a mile or two. We‚Äôve been driving all night, at first so excited we talked in hushed, urgent voices, like we were sharing a secret. Everybody was congratulating or joshing Barry about his newfound freedom. It took a long time for the girls to fall asleep.   
Now it‚Äôs just me and Barry, my hand hanging on the seat behind him, silent, taking in the view. The interstate is stretching ahead, miles and miles of cracked asphalt under a giant sunrise. Something, I don‚Äôt know what, catches my attention, a sound perhaps. I look back and I see Alice in the back seat, all freckles and paint, and a pain I never felt before goes coursing through my veins. I think, *I‚Äôve never seen anyone so pretty*. I think, *I‚Äôve never been happier*. I think, *I‚Äôve never been so miserable*.
