	‚ÄúDon‚Äôt you people make yourselves too comfortable,‚Äù says Joanne as she‚Äôs making herself comfortable on my person. I feel the weight on her head on my belly. I put my hand on her wet hair. ‚ÄúEddie told me the food will be coming soon. Hey, have you guys met Eddie?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúI‚Äôm sorry to say we have,‚Äù says Barry. ‚ÄúWhat kind of food are we talking about?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúJohnses, have you followed the band around all the way since Charleston?‚Äù asks Cindy, ever the talk-show host.  
	‚ÄúWe sure have,‚Äù says Joanne. ‚ÄúSure have. We love it out here on the road. You can say that John and I, we‚Äôre a little Steam Pillars fan club of our own.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúThe Charleston fan club,‚Äù says Cindy. ‚ÄúThe little delegation from the Carolinas.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúA fan club of two,‚Äù says John. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm not sure the good people of Charleston would approve of our undertaking. People back home, if they saw us here‚Ä¶‚Äù He lets out a long whistle. ‚ÄúThey‚Äôd tar and feather us and drive us out of town.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúI was thinking the same thing,‚Äù says Barry. ‚ÄúMy father, he‚Äôs got an old German Karabiner rifle hanging above the mantlepiece. He got it in the war.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúMy father wouldn‚Äôt bother with a gun. He‚Äôd strangle me with his bare hands,‚Äù says Patty. I lift my head and realize that our little huddle has become a big one: Shirley and Veronica and Patty to our sides, the Joneses in the middle, and at the edge, more hugs and cuddles from people whose names we don‚Äôt know yet.    
	The sound of people playing in the river, the touch of skin on my skin, the wind in the trees are all a lullaby. The sun bounces off the water and plays on my face. I let my shades fall over my eyes and drift into a deep, satisfying sleep. At the edge of my consciousness I think I hear Eddie‚Ä¶

*(‚ÄúAlright boys and girls, negros and Italians, the food is here so I need some people to help carry it over‚Ä¶ let‚Äôs get cooking‚Ä¶ Alice, stop touching that‚Ä¶ Veronica, do your magic and light a fire‚Ä¶‚Äù)*

I wake up to the smell of roasting chicken and cornmeal patties frying, to the chatter of people making food together. 

*(Alice\! Stop spilling stuff on the ground\!)*

*(Here, try this, tell me it isn‚Äôt better than your mama‚Äôs.)*

*(Anybody got a fork I could borrow?)*

And then:

*(Honk-honk-honk\!)*

‚ÄúIsn‚Äôt it Pete honking?‚Äù I hear Cindy saying. ‚ÄúDo you think he‚Äôs hungry?‚Äù  
I rub my face. In front of us, next to the river, on a makeshift table, covered in a tattered tablecloth, Eleanore is leading a little kitchen brigade of slicers and mixers. Next to a dim, smoky fire, Eddie and Mucho and a few others are propping garden hoes, flipping little Johnny-cakes on their flat blades‚Ä¶ holding whole chickens, skewered on sloping, green branches‚Ä¶ balancing coffee pots on smooth rocks next to the fire.   
‚ÄúHere,‚Äù says Eleanore. She gives me a steaming, soggy cardboard shoebox (‚ÄúKyle of London, men‚Äôs size 13‚Äù). I peek inside and see a half-burnt whole chicken, some cornmeal patties the shape of garden hoe blades, a couple of boiled eggs.

(*Honk-honk-honk\!)*

Cindy and I walk through the orchard to the carpark, carrying food. Three men stand next to the chicken-wire fence, watching: Pete and Mo the bus driver and Heath, the white piano player.  
‚ÄúWho‚Äôs hungry?‚Äù I say. I expect them to rush over, but they all have their attention turned to something beyond the fence‚Ä¶  
	‚ÄúSo there you are,‚Äù says Pete, not shifting his gaze.   
‚ÄúTrouble?‚Äù asks Cindy, finding a place next to them, looking at the same direction.  
‚ÄúI think so,‚Äù says Pete. ‚ÄúLook over there, you fellas.‚Äù  
Beyond the trees, over on the highway, I can see a black-and-white Chevy Bel Air parked on the shoulder. Its owner stands next to it and seems to be watching us. Across the distance I can see the sun reflected on a pair of chrome sunglasses.  
‚ÄúIt‚Äôs definitely the same guy I saw,‚Äù says Heath. He‚Äôs got a raspy, high, musical voice. ‚ÄúI recognize that car. The black-and-white car that looks like a cop-car.‚Äù He‚Äôs turning to look at Cindy above the rim of his shades.   
‚ÄúHi,‚Äù Cindy says to him. ‚ÄúI love your piano playing.‚Äù  
‚ÄúTry to concentrate, man,‚Äù says Mo. ‚ÄúSame car, you sure?‚Äù  
‚ÄúSame car, same car,‚Äù says Heath, still looking at Cindy.  
Over at the highway, the little figure seems to be watching us. I fight the temptation to wave and see if he would wave back.  
‚ÄúOkay,‚Äù says Mo. ‚ÄúLet‚Äôs haul ass.‚Äù  
He walks over to the bus, climbs the stairs and honks the horn again.  
Pete says, ‚ÄúSomeone had better run over and‚Äì‚Äù  
But Cindy is already halfway through the orchard.  
We stand there, watching the man who watches us.  
‚ÄúSo,‚Äù I say to Heath, ‚Äúlove your piano playing, man.‚Äù  
Heath eyes me and walks away, towards the cars.  
‚ÄúHe doesn‚Äôt seem to be big on talking,‚Äù Pete says.

A big column of steam rises above the orchard, like someone poured water over a fire. People start streaming through the trees, carrying shoeboxes and brown paper bags, hurried, silent. Eddie comes along, panting.  
	‚ÄúNo mixed cars\!‚Äù he calls at people. ‚ÄúWhites with whites\! Blacks with blacks\!‚Äù  
	The Joneses scurry by, hurriedly putting on each other‚Äôs clothes. Cindy and Eleanore usher people into their cars. Barry comes after them, balancing a tower of shoeboxes in his hands. The top one falls over and a big fried chicken falls out of it. Everywhere it smells of warm food and wet cardboard.  
	‚ÄúLeave it\!‚Äù says Eleanore. ‚ÄúLet‚Äôs move.‚Äù  
	Eddie‚Äôs friends are traffic cops: I see the cigarette girl taking position near the gate, knocking on the nearest car‚Äôs hood, calling, ‚ÄúYou drive through there\! Don‚Äôt follow that car, you need to go in different directions\!‚Äù  
	‚ÄúStart your engines\!‚Äù  
	‚ÄúBurn some rubber\!‚Äù  
	‚ÄúWe meet in Greensboro tomorrow morning\!‚Äù  
	People pop open trunks, shove food into them, stumble over each other as they run into their cars.  
	Eddie yells, ‚ÄúHeath, get into Barry‚Äôs car\!‚Äù  
	‚ÄúWho‚Äôs Barry?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúThe wet nerd over there\!‚Äù	  
	‚ÄúWhere‚Äôs Alice? Where‚Äôs Alice\!‚Äù  
	‚ÄúShe went with the Joneses\! Let‚Äôs go\!‚Äù

## Track 8: I Saw It Too  
*(Isn‚Äôt it a beautiful day to be a-rocking and a-rolling, America? Yes, the Soviets are sending guard dogs into space, to float above our heads‚Ä¶ but today we can look at the sky, that clear blue sky, America, and remember His grace always shines on us. Anyway, here‚Äôs an oldie from a fallen giant of rock‚Äôn‚Äôroll‚Ä¶ a naughty boy indeed‚Ä¶ Jerry Lee, we don‚Äôt know where you went to, if you‚Äôre hiding or only resting, but your magical piano fingers are dearly missed here on the airwaves‚Ä¶*

*Craaaaheyzeee aaaaaaarms that reach to ho-hold somebody newwww‚Ä¶)*

I‚Äôm driving. Barry sits next to me. Eleanore and Cindy sit in the back, flanking Heath. His hands are clasped in his lap and he appears to be praying. With his jet-black hair and dark sunglasses and his cheap, dark suit, he looks like Eleanore‚Äôs masculine mirror image. He‚Äôs got a sad face; as he prays, his lips move as if to the words from the song on the radio. In the blazing sun it seems his eyes are shut behind his shades. He‚Äôs got a big nose and a small mouth that pouts when it isn‚Äôt moving.   
The caravan of painted cars is nowhere to be seen. It‚Äôs just us on the road.   
	‚ÄúSo,‚Äù says Eleanore. ‚ÄúThis is wild, don‚Äôt you think, Mr. Heath? All the chasing and the running around? Are you excited too?‚Äù  
	In the rearview mirror, Heath looks anything other than excited. He doesn‚Äôt answer.  
‚ÄúWe‚Äôre big fans, Mr. Heath,‚Äù says Cindy. ‚ÄúHave you met everybody? This is‚Äì‚Äù  
‚ÄúIt‚Äôs Mr. Caine,‚Äù says Heath. ‚ÄúActually. It‚Äôs Heath Walker Cain.‚Äù His voice is quiet, controlled ‚Äì a Kentucky voice. His attention wanders to the gold in Cindy‚Äôs hair.   
‚ÄúYour piano playing is so *gas*, Mr. Caine,‚Äù I say. ‚ÄúI mean you make that damn piano sound just like an anvil, you know what I‚Äôm saying, Mr. Caine?‚Äù  
Heath smiles and doesn‚Äôt answer. Then his little smile fades into a little pout. His eyes stay on Cindy‚Äôs hair. 

*(myyyy‚Ä¶ troubledmiiind‚Ä¶ was bluuu‚Äìhuue‚Ä¶)*

Cindy draws closer to Heath.   
	‚ÄúIs there a Missus Caine?‚Äù she asks. Heath backs away from her, accidentally touches Eleanore‚Äôs knee, then backs forward. His knuckles are white in his lap.  
	‚ÄúIs it warm in here?‚Äù he says.  
Cindy relents. She rolls down the window and sticks her head out. I stare at the blue-tinted mountains ahead, the fields engulfing us.	  
	‚ÄúDamnit‚Äì‚Äù says Cindy. She pulls her head back. ‚ÄúI lost my headband to the wind.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúLanguage,‚Äù says Eleanore. I notice as she‚Äôs putting her hand on Heath‚Äôs knee. Heath winces; Eleanore removes her hand. 

*(Vreeeem\! Hup-hup-hup-hup-hup‚Ä¶)* 

‚ÄúNow, we‚Äôve got a little company,‚Äù Barry says. ‚ÄúFriends of yours, Mr. Caine?‚Äù  
	In the rearview mirror I see a small band of motorcyclists riding in a tight cluster, wearing matching denim jackets, all riding brand-new Indians. They split to flank us. One of them is holding something red in his hand‚Ä¶ Heath is visibly rattled.   
	‚ÄúDon‚Äôt stop, kid,‚Äù he tells me.  
	The lead biker finds his position in front of us. On the back of his vest there‚Äôs an embroidered sign, like a mirror image of the figure 7\. He slows down and I step on the brakes with a trembling foot. The rest of the riders surround us.   
	‚ÄúThis is gonna be a real *hoot*,‚Äù says Eleanore.   
	One of the bikers sticks his head through the window, looking at us. His face is covered in a black bandana. Heath is frozen.   
	‚ÄúYou people maybe‚Ä¶‚Äù says the biker. The something red he‚Äôs holding is waving in the wind.  
	‚ÄúRoll up the window, for *crissake*,‚Äù mumbles Heath.  
	‚ÄúOh sweet mother, fuck this to hell,‚Äù says Eleanore.  
	‚ÄúLanguage,‚Äù says Cindy, the only one not rattled. ‚ÄúHow may we help you, sir?‚Äù  
	The biker says, ‚ÄúYou people maybe lose a handkerchief?" He shoves his hand through the window, holding Cindy‚Äôs red headband.   
	Cindy smiles. She takes the headband. ‚ÄúMuch obliged,‚Äù she says. ‚ÄúYou guys are local?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúDon‚Äôt converse with them, you fool,‚Äù Heath mumbles. The biker turns to look at him. The rest of the bikers keep a tight formation around the car. My foot itches on the gas as I try to keep the car at a steady speed.  
	‚ÄúYou passing through?‚Äù says the biker, keeping his gaze centered on Heath. ‚ÄúYankees?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúGod forbid,‚Äù Cindy says, elongating her vowels.   
	‚ÄúYou kids should look after yourselves out here on the road,‚Äù the biker says, his head almost inside our car. ‚ÄúWe‚Äôve had some *bad elements* passing through today.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúBad elements,‚Äù Eleanore says, choked.  
	‚ÄúYeah,‚Äù says the biker. ‚ÄúWhy, Jeremy here saw some of them riding an old schoolbus. He says they were n‚Äì‚Äù

*(Honk-honk-honk\!)*

The biker looks back. Then all the bikers look back. Without a word, they step on the gas and overtake us, exhausts and tires smoking.   
	‚ÄúDid we just get saved?‚Äù Barry says.  
	Behind us I see a black-and-white Chevy Bel Air.   
	‚ÄúJust the opposite, Bear,‚Äù says Cindy, turning to look back. She catches Heath looking at her and smiles at him. He looks away.  
	I say, ‚ÄúIs it the same guy, Cindy?‚Äù   
	‚ÄúYeah,‚Äù Cindy says. ‚ÄúChrome shades, the whole package. Wouldn‚Äôt you agree, Heath? Mr. Caine, I mean.‚Äù	  
	Heath loses his cool. ‚ÄúWill you just step on that gas already, kid?‚Äù he yells at me.   
	Eleanore snorts. I step on the gas. The black and white car speeds up and keeps a steady distance, far behind us. Cindy finishes tying her headband around her hair and turns and sticks her body out of the window. In the side mirror I can see her waving and shouting at the car behind us.  
	‚ÄúAre you crazy?‚Äù Barry yells at her. ‚ÄúGet yourself back in the vehicle\! Now\!‚Äù  
	The wind carries Cindy‚Äôs voice back to me: ‚ÄúHey friend\! Friend\! You want us? Come on by\!‚Äù  
	I hold my breath. The chasing car slows down. Then it makes a turn and disappears off of the rear view mirror. Cindy pulls herself back in, red-faced and excited.   
	‚ÄúYou‚Äôve gone insane, girl,‚Äù Eleanore says.  
	‚ÄúYou‚Äôve got a death wish, kiddo,‚Äù I say.   
	‚ÄúJust keeping you guys safe,‚Äù says Cindy. She smiles at Heath and puts his hand on her knee.  
We catch up to Moe‚Äôs faded-yellow schoolbus. I honk and he opens the door.  
	‚ÄúDid you see him?‚Äù I shout at Moe.  
	‚ÄúDid you?‚Äù Moe shouts back.   
	‚ÄúHe was chasing us awhile back,‚Äù I shout. ‚ÄúA bunch of bikers were hard on our tail too ‚Äì I think they‚Äôre looking for you guys\!‚Äù  
	Pete stands next to Moe, thinking. ‚ÄúI think we‚Äôre gonna all go back in tight formation\!‚Äù he shouts at me. ‚ÄúThere‚Äôs strength in numbers.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúStrength in what?‚Äù   
	‚ÄúIn numbers\!‚Äù he yells back. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs what the Book says\!‚Äù  
	‚ÄúThe Good Book?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúThe Green Book\!‚Äù  
	‚ÄúWhat book?‚Äù  
	Mo and Pete exchange glances. ‚ÄúWhite kids‚Äì‚Äù Moe says.   
	‚ÄúHey,‚Äù I shout. ‚ÄúDid you guys see our Alice?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúHey,‚Äù says Cindy. ‚ÄúShe‚Äôs *my* Alice.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúI think she was with the other white couple\!‚Äù Moe says.   
	‚ÄúThe Joneses?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúThey‚Äôre up ahead‚Äì‚Äù Moe says. ‚ÄúPassed us a little while back\!‚Äù  
	Eleanore says, ‚ÄúDo you think we left her there at the river? What if she drowned?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúEleanore\!‚Äù I yell. ‚ÄúCome on, don‚Äôt talk like that\! Where the hell is she?‚Äù  
	Eleanore gives me an inquisitive look. I turn around and shut up. I think of losing Alice, even before I had a chance to tell her how I feel. My skin grows hot.   
	‚ÄúYou okay there, Timmy?‚Äù Barry asks.   
	‚ÄúDon‚Äôt call me that,‚Äù I say, irked.   
	‚ÄúTim, listen to me,‚Äù says Heath. He puts his hand on my shoulder. ‚ÄúYour girlfriend is okay. She‚Äôs in one of the other cars. Maybe she‚Äôs with the band. Maybe they took her.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúShe isn‚Äôt his *girlfriend*,‚Äù Cindy says, irked.  
	‚ÄúNot yet, at least,‚Äù Eleanore mumbles.   
	‚ÄúWhat do you mean by that?‚Äù Cindy says.  
	‚ÄúShe‚Äôs not your girlfriend?‚Äù Heath says. ‚ÄúYou sound awfully worried.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúWell,‚Äù I say. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs just that Barry here promised her mother‚Äì‚Äù  
	‚ÄúYe‚Äìe‚Äìah,‚Äù Heath says.  
	‚ÄúSuppose,‚Äù says Eleanore, ‚Äúthat this man in the black-and-white car took her.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúPuppy\!‚Äù Cindy shouts. ‚ÄúNow you‚Äôre making *me* worried\!‚Äù  
   
*(butnooooow I‚Äômmmm so lonely‚Ä¶ oh-all the time‚Ä¶)*

Our caravan, three cars long, is parking along the the US-360. Mo, Pete and Barry are out, pissing. The Joneses didn‚Äôt have Alice, it would seem, but they did say they definitely saw her along the way. The Joneses are older than us, a married couple, literally grown-ups. They seem trustworthy to me.   
	‚ÄúYeah, we saw her with Baba, do you know Baba?‚Äù says Joanne to me while the three cars are parking on the side of the road. We‚Äôre leaning on the trunk of the Joneses‚Äô car, sharing a cigarette.  
	‚ÄúHe‚Äôs the dude from Africa. The Mad Drummer. I think he‚Äôs the soul of the band,‚Äù she says, ‚Äúits essence.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúI know who it is,‚Äù I say. ‚ÄúWe sort of gave him a ride at one point.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúWhat do you mean, ‚Äòsorta‚Äô?‚Äù she says, laughing.   
	‚ÄúAh, you don‚Äôt wanna know. Anyway, Alice‚Ä¶‚Äù  
	‚ÄúI saw her helping him carry something over to the wagon, then come on board with him. You know their wagon, the Steam Pillars. An old army truck.‚Äù She‚Äôs got a pearl necklace in pink, and long, pink fingernails, the shade of which I find annoying. But her voice is soft in this soft, endless afternoon.  
	Barry comes back. I pass him the cigarette as he comes near.  
	‚ÄúAnyway,‚Äù says Joanne, ‚ÄúI‚Äôm sure Alice‚Äôs safe with Baba. Anyone still up for a malted?‚Äù

Our caravan is now five cars long: us, the Joneses, Pete and Mo, Shirley and her friends, and another van, featuring a fantastic painting of a lark and three guys we don‚Äôt know yet. We‚Äôre all joking about how we ride together to find Alice, like a posse, and we make up secret codenames and pretend we‚Äôre in the Old West. But inside I do feel nervous: it‚Äôs getting increasingly dark outside.   
	‚ÄúWe can‚Äôt do Malteds together\!‚Äù Pete yells from the bus window. He‚Äôs sitting on the bench facing us, like kids do on these busses on field trips. ‚ÄúWho do you think will seat you and me together?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúThat‚Äôs a good point,‚Äù I say. ‚ÄúI do look like a rough customer.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúYeah,‚Äù says Pete. ‚ÄúWith your ugly mug. You could sour milk with that face. Any-way, let‚Äôs turn to our Green Book. Here it is. Moore's Drive-In Restaurant.‚Äù

We‚Äôre in the All-Nite store, waiting on some cigarettes. Next to the curb there‚Äôs a few cars already parked and waitresses walking between them. A little sign next to the door says they‚Äôre Open ALL Nite. A waitress bends by a near car and as I stare they turn on their lights: yellow against the blue of the adjacent U-Park-It Self-Park terminal, five stories high..  
	‚ÄúIt‚Äôs a survival guide, is what it is,‚Äù says Pete. He hands me a green-colored booklet. ‚ÄúYou buy this now, you‚Äôll be ready for any survival scenario.‚Äù  
	The guy at the counter brings us two packs of Buzz. Remembering Barry‚Äôs advice at the Dan river, I also buy a packet of Moon Pies.  
	‚ÄúSurvival for what? The Wild? The Wild West?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúNah, man, no. Just for being a black man in a white man‚Äôs world.‚Äù He stares at his shadow and mine.   
‚ÄúIt‚Äôs like, if you wanna join ‚Äì not just, you know, not just for the weekend. For the long haul. If you want to do that you need to travel like we do. You need to know what we know.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúLike what?‚Äù I say.   
	‚ÄúLike,‚Äù says Pete, ‚Äúthe most important tip written in this book. Listen man, I‚Äôm not kidding‚Äî‚Äù but I never find out what the most important tip in the book is, because a moment later someone behind us yells, ‚ÄúAlice\! She‚Äôs here\!‚Äù and someone else yells, ‚ÄúSo you‚Äôre Alice\!‚Äù and someone yells, ‚ÄúThe Posse has found Alice\!‚Äù and some other people cheer together, ‚ÄúAlice\!‚Äù  
	The four of us run towards her: pink-haired pixie sitting cross-legged in the back side of the band truck. Babatunde stands, regal, beside her. She‚Äôs drumming on the floor of the truck and Babatunde corrects her:  
	‚ÄúNo, like this, like this\!‚Äù  
	‚ÄúMine\!‚Äù says Cindy. ‚ÄúShe‚Äôs mine\! Just mine\!‚Äù she runs at the truck, climbs nimbly on board and hugs Alice, buries her face in Alice‚Äôs shoulder. ‚ÄúJust mine\!‚Äù  
	Alice hugs her back. She steals a look at me. ‚ÄúDid you miss me?‚Äù she asks, facing Cindy.  
	‚ÄúYes,‚Äù I say. Alice looks back up.   
	‚ÄúWe all missed you dearly,‚Äù I smile. She smiles back.  
	‚ÄúI thought you drowned,‚Äù says Eleanore.   
	‚ÄúI knew my Cindy will be back to save me. I wasn‚Äôt afraid at all,‚Äù says Alice, pecking Cindy on the cheek. She looks up at me. ‚ÄúI knew you‚Äôd be back,‚Äù she says. 

Almost all of the parking spaces beneath Moore‚Äôs red sign are taken up by our caravan. Barry and the Girls are standing next to the pay phone, with Pete and Eddie, eating Bird Dogs in hot dog buns.   
	‚ÄúAnyway,‚Äù says Pete, squeezing ketchup into his bun, ‚Äúthe point I was trying to make is that Elvis ‚Äì he‚Äôs just a performer, right? Jerry-Lee, on the other hand ‚Äì he‚Äôs an innovator. He makes up his own music, his own kind of playing.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúYou‚Äôre insane,‚Äù I say. ‚ÄúEverybody knows there can only be one King.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúKings are men, too,‚Äù says Eddie. He looks at Barry as he says that. He takes a long drag from his cigarette.   
	"Elvis is like an old-school king,‚Äù I say. ‚ÄúGoes to fight for his country. Goes to the army, man, for the greater good.‚Äù  
	‚ÄúIt‚Äôs a stunt,‚Äù Eleanore sneers.   
	‚ÄúIt‚Äôs not\!‚Äù  
	Pete says, ‚ÄúJerry-Lee goes with his art, wherever it takes him. Did you hear at that one show, he lit his piano on fire?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúI told you, he‚Äôs crazy,‚Äù I say.   
	‚ÄúHe‚Äôs got fire in him,‚Äù Alice suddenly says. We all look at her.   
	‚ÄúI mean,‚Äù she says. ‚ÄúHe‚Äôs got fire in him. It‚Äôs in him. You can hear that in his playing. It‚Äôs just bursting to get out.‚Äù  
	I laugh. ‚ÄúHow would you know it?‚Äù   
	She says, ‚ÄúBy listening to him.‚Äù She blushes. ‚ÄúA lot.‚Äù  
	Cindy asks Alice, ‚ÄúDo you think you have fire inside you?‚Äù  
	‚ÄúI do,‚Äù says Alice. ‚ÄúThe show showed me that. This music.‚Äù She looks at me. ‚ÄúTim and Pete have got that fire too, this is why they go to all these underground shows.‚Äù Her words send pleasant tingles down my back.  
	Eddie asks, ‚ÄúDo you girls have fire in you, too?‚Äù  
	Cindy and Eleanore look at each other and laugh.  
	‚ÄúYeah, they got a little bit of it going on, too,‚Äù Alice laughs with them. ‚ÄúCindy is the powder keg, and Eleanore‚Äôs the spark.‚Äù  
	Eddie says, ‚ÄúAnd what about Barry here?‚Äù  
	Barry says, ‚ÄúLeave me alone, Edwar-do.‚Äù  
	Eddie says, ‚ÄúYou look like you would give your life to a higher cause. Wouldn‚Äôt even hesitate.‚Äù  
	Barry laughs, ‚ÄúIs the cause dancing at the Steam Pillars shows? If so, I‚Äôm willing to give my life to that.‚Äù  
	Cindy says, ‚ÄúIs that Barry talking over there? I mean the voice sounds like his, but the words‚Äì‚Äù  
Eddie says, ‚ÄúYou know what, all of you guys, come with me. There‚Äôs someone you should meet.‚Äù

## Track 9: Katyushka, the Deadly Assassin

## Track 11: Nine Minutes Past Four
*Hey there, listener Jane, hi there, listener John. Come on by the radio, and sit down. Your friend, DJ Jonah, has some unpleasant bit of news to dispense. ‚Äã	"Now I get the worst part," I say. "I will never, ever, see him on stage. Never."

When we heard the news on the radio, only a few hours earlier, it didn't come as words but as a dull buzzing in my ears. The road was lit but my eyes stopped transmitting what they saw. An old bronze sign dedicated to a Revolutionary general, green and flaking, made the place feel even more ancient. The road sloped down and turned into a wooden bridge. The air was dead, no echoes. *(are you guys ready for something NEW?)*

The door of the projectionist booth opens and an orange-and-white clad man comes out. Eddie lifts his head towards him. The man winks at Eddie and goes away, around the building, towards the concession stand.   
‚Äã	All around us the speakers explodes with sound. Jo‚Äôs guitar is a tinny thunder. The kids in the car next to me are stunned. I look as one of them turns to look at me. I smile at him to let him know he hasn‚Äôt gone crazy. Now Vilma‚Äôs voice is loud over the guitar‚Ä¶  

*(d‚Äôwarden threwaparty in coun-TEE jail\!)*

In the back, we see the Joneses get out of their car and dance together. Up ahead, we see Cindy and Eleanore and Barry do the same. On screen, the figures of the dancers, all wearing inmates' uniforms, and that of the live players, all dancing too, blend and move into each other. It seems the band is playing with the movie instead of on top of it. The music is hot, and the people in the car next to us start swaying in their seats with it.    
‚Äã	‚ÄúWho the hell are these people?‚Äù the driver yells through the open window. Eddie got the speakers to work really loud now, and the whole lot is buzzing with Pillars music.  
‚Äã	‚ÄúThey‚Äôre called The Steam Pillars,‚Äù Alice tells him. ‚ÄúThey got white and colored folks playing together and they‚Äôre the hottest thing around\! Hey, aren't you tired of being cooped up in your car? Do you dare to dance?‚Äù

*(y‚Äôcain‚Äôt findapadneh use a wood-den chaiir, let‚Äôs‚Äì)*

The Pillars‚Äô version of *Jailhouse Rock* is angry, not funny at all. Vilma‚Äôs voice breaks and commands attention and Babatunde‚Äôs drums are driving forward at a slow pace. Their version is everything I feel since I heard the news: betrayal, confusion; a loud, boisterous mourning.   
‚Äã	We walk hand in hand between cars, just Alice and me. When we reach a couple who dances, we stop and dance with them.
‚Äã	"Who are they? They rock!" one of them asks. 
‚Äã	"They're the Steam Pillars!" Alice says. "Hey, you guys, don‚Äôt stay stuck in your car. Here, take some matches. There's a clue in them."
‚Äã	As we walk away, I say in her ear, "Real smooth, Katyashka," and she giggles and squeezes me. 
‚Äã
"I'm out of matches," I say her after a while. 
‚Äã	"I've got one book left," she says. "Oh my, Mama's going to be mad."
‚Äã	"We should give it to someone special!" I say. "Really *choose* someone, you know?"
‚Äã	‚ÄúThe problem is,‚Äù Alice says in my ear, ‚Äúhow do we choose? They‚Äôre *all* dancing\!‚Äù  
‚Äã	I look around to see the whole lot is out of their cars, dancing. People turned on their headlights and the whole lot is now illuminated by those glaring, yellow lights. Some people dance on the roofs of their cars. The air is dense. We see Rosetta going by, and she's bursting with laughter. She winks at us. 
‚Äã	"Lord," she says, "Mama gonna be mad."

*(BOOM!)*

The song ends abruptly: there‚Äôs trouble on stage. Then a gunshot ‚Äì or is it a muffler backfiring? I find myself running towards the stage with Alice, but as we get near Cindy she signals us to stop.
‚Äã	==‚ÄúI‚Äôm going to kill them,‚Äù says B.L.==  
‚Äã	‚ÄúWe‚Äôll sort it out,‚Äù says Cindy. They push their way towards the truck.   
‚Äã	On the truck bed, Mucho and two of ==B.L.‚Äôs biker friends== are brawling. Heath stands between Vilma and another ‚ÄúSeven‚Äù, as more of them circle the truck in their bikes. The crowd is ablaze. One of the bikers grabs one of the mics and calls into it, 

*(ladies and gentleman, I am Captain Whitaker of the South Carolina Citizen‚Äôs Decency League. We apologize for the disruption you have suffered and we will make sure to resume the motion picture moment‚Äì)*

We see Vilma as she grabs the mic and lays it down close to her face. Mucho has joined Heath in creating a perimeter around her. Through the nearby speakers we hear her voice, enormous even now, as she cries out,

*(Do you all wanna watch the rest of the ‚Äòmotion picture...‚Äô Or do you guys want some rock‚Äôn‚ÄôROLL?)*

The crowd cheers; it's a long, rolling rumble, going through the lot like a wave. When the wave reaches us we allow ourselves to be swept along with it, and we hold hands and shout and scream. 
‚Äã	"Rock'n'roll will never die!" Alice yells at the screen, at the image of dead Elvis. 
‚Äã	The truck is swaying and rocking -- the denim-clad gang that tried to seize the mics is leaving the stage. Under the truck I see Cindy, talking earnestly with one of the members, holding on to his top button, her face flushed. A huge "boo" greets the bikers as they leave. ‚Äã	
‚Äã	‚ÄúHave you ever heard a crowd so loud?‚Äù Alice shouts. Her eyes are twinkling. I hold her and kiss her, gently, lovingly. In that moment, for the first and only time, I think I see something. As I watch the shadows the band casts on the screen, they seem to shift shapes and grow wings. Some winged shadows fly off the screen, and some remain hovering over us all. 
‚Äã	It ends as soon as it starts. I will never have visions again. 

*(Heath, why don't you sing one? Come on, Heath, give us a number...)*

We walk towards the stage. You almost can't see the cars now, the crowd is so thick. Every now and then we see someone dancing on their car, as if on an island in the deep sea. 
‚Äã	I say, "Do you think Cindy got rid of the Sevens?"
‚Äã	"I'm sure she did," Alice says, pulling on my hand. "Come on! I want to be where it's *at*..."
‚Äã	On the truck bed, it seems Vilma and Babatunde are trying to convince Heath. Mucho comes over holding a microphone stand and puts it in front of Heath's face. We're not far off from the makeshift stage now, and we can hear Heath clearly now, not through the little drive-in speakers...

*(Alright, alright... Ladies and gentleman, this is a song dear to my heart - it's also Elvis's greatest hit - let's take it away in C-major, Baba, Mucho, try to follow my rhythm...)

*(ta-tata-TA!)*

Heath jabs the piano keys four times, hard, and the crowd grows silent. We watch with baited breath: Heath takes his time. 
‚Äã	Then, his fingers start traveling on the keys, softly, and the air is full of a soft, lyrical notes. Heath croons softly into the microphone...

*(Y-y-you shake my nerves and you rattle my brain...)*

Now Heath issues the same four notes, but now they sound sad and longing. Heath and his piano are a diminutive figures under the hulking image of Elvis. He almost whispers into the mic...

*(too much a-love drives a man insane...)*

The piano picks up rhythm and speed. I look around; the faces around us are expectant. Everybody sways with Heath's playing like reeds. Next to us an older woman, very pretty, is mouthing the words with the music. Elvis is a fancy-suited, black-and-white figure above us. 

*(y-y-you broke my will -- but what a thrill --)*

Then the movie reel runs out and the screen fills with splattering, flashing light... the screen is blank now except for the giant shadows of the Steam Pillars. On a dime, Heath's piano and the drums and the bass and the guitar all join in on the same beat... 

*(good-NESS grAI-cious! greAT balls of FI-er!)*

An huge cheer rolls over us. The crowd is enormous; its ends are infinite. Heath hammers on the piano like a madman. 
‚Äã	"Who is this guy?" says someone next to us.
‚Äã	"Heath Walker Caine," says someone next to them. It turns out to be the male Jones, who winks at us as he says, "Hey, dare to dance?"
‚Äã	Once it is discovered we're in the know, people have a lot of questions for us: 
‚Äã	"Why do you fellas paint on your cars?"
‚Äã	"So it's not a hoax. Are you sure?"
‚Äã	"What are they called again? Steaming Dinner?"
‚Äã	Alice has stopped dancing; she's standing and watching Heath play, mesmerized. I hug her and she says, "I was so stupid! I get it now!"
‚Äã	I say, "Get what?"

*(TWANGGGGGGG!)*

*(Damn it. Hey, do any of you people know how to change an electrical bass string?)*
‚Äã
"Never mind. I bet *you* can change a string," Alice says. 
‚Äã	"Of course I can change a string," I say. I rush forward, holding my hand up. The song goes on; Heath's left hand is working frantically now. Jo's guitar remains in the background, respectful of Heath's piano. As I rush through the crowd I can  smell the electricity in the air. Almost all of the car owners have turned on their headlights; almost every standing space is full of people dancing in those lights. 

*(s'alright, Eddie, you can stay where you are. I can see Tommmy here coming to the stage... greeeeeaaaaat Balls of Fire!)*

I climb on to the bed of the truck. Wires, thick as railroad steel, issue from the truck and into a whirring, buzzing, smelly crate on the ground. The Steam Pillars stand shoulder-to-shoulder on the truck bed, hemmed in by microphone stands and amplifiers. In the middle, Heath goes to town on his small upright piano. He plays it so hard the truck sways, and we sway with it. I make my way towards Mucho, who decided to keep playing on his remaining three strings, and find myself sandwiched between him and Vilma. 
‚Äã	"Hey kid, string's in the case," Mucho says.
‚Äã	"Hey kid, knew you'd be back," says Vilma and gives me a nudge. It's dark and messy on the floor. I duck and rummage in the guitar case, trying to gauge the right string with my fingers. 
‚Äã	"It's just that it's new," Mucho says, facing away from the mic. "Mama got it for me."
‚Äã	"It's beautiful!" I say. I pull out a coiled spring, as thick as my little finger. "Is that a Sunburst Precision?"
‚Äã	"I don't give a fuck! Are you changing my string?"
‚Äã	"While you're playing?!"
‚Äã	He laughs. "Welcome to the big league, son."
‚Äã	Mucho, a whole foot taller than me, keeps dancing and swaying with his bass, not missing a beat, while I try to put the string through the socket, to stretch it, lock it around the tuners. The second I get it right, Mucho pushes me away, turns on the tuning peg and continues playing. I wipe my forehead. It's incredibly hot on stage as everyone steals themselves for the last big refrain, and when it comes Vilma says off-mic,
‚Äã	"Fuck you, Heath. You went and took the wind under my sails."
‚Äã	Mucho laughs. "Don't blaspheme in front of the priest," he mocks. Heath is literally dripping with sweat. His black curls fall from the neat hairdo and float around his face. He's standing above the piano, hammering with incredible speed and force, bellowing into the microphone. He lifts his head, ecstatic, sweating; he looks back; he winks at me. 
‚Äã	Behind Mucho, Joe is playing harder now, moving with her guitar as if shaking and rattling it is what makes the music fall out of it. I stare at her fingers moving across the strings...

*(c'mon bahy-be... drive me crahy-zee...)*

‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚ÄãMucho puts his hand on the mic and says, "Nice work, kid."
‚Äã	"Yeah," says Vilma. "You can dance with me, don't be shy, boy. Hey, when did you learn to change strings that way?"
‚Äã	"I didn't," I say. "My first time." I dance with her. 
‚Äã	Mucho bellows a laugh so loud it makes me cringe. "Hey kid, you want a job with the band? Spend some time in the limelight?"
‚Äã	I look up at the limelight and for the first time I see the audience: an enormous sea of faces and hands, with no beginning or end, all illuminated by car headlights, and moving as if they were one body, moving in exact coordination with the music; with the movement of the Steam Pillars around me; with the creaking suspension of the truck. An enormous feeling brews in my stomach. 
‚Äã	Without knowing it, I lift my arms and the sea of people near us cheers... in the crowd, Alice is sending me excited kisses and turns away almost immediately to dance with a stranger. 
‚Äã	Mucho says to the mic,

*(Yeah! Give it to our new roadie, Tommy!)*

Later, in the band's truck leaving the drive-in, with Eddie smoking at the wheel and the band all celebrating and drinking, I turn and look back. 
‚Äã	In the future experts will debate endlessly on when was it that the Steam Pillars first burst into public attention. Some will say Atlanta, others, that it was as early as Columbia -- but for me it's always going to be this moment, driving away from the Hitching Post Drive-In in Salisbury, when I look behind us and see the length of US-29 is an uninterrupted string of  caravan headlights, running up and down the hills and extending into the horizon. 

## Track 12: Sundown Town
